# Tahita Bulmer's Intro

> “On Noel's team tonight, the leader of Mercury-nominated New Young Pony Club. She's so cool. None of you squares probably know who she is, but I do, because a researcher told me. It's Tahita Bulmer.”

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction blends faux-hipness with self-deprecating irony, aiming to lampoon both the host’s persona and the show's audience. The joke lies in the juxtaposition of the host pretending to be “in the know” while simultaneously admitting ignorance.

Let’s break it down:

- “She’s so cool. None of you squares probably know who she is…” sets up the speaker (host) as someone who is aligned with the cool, in-the-know elite — unlike the implied unfashionable audience (“squares”). This is a mockingly pretentious setup.
  
- “…but I do, because a researcher told me” is the punchline. The host undercuts his own bluff by admitting he only knows because someone else told him — an admission that nullifies the earlier attempt at being hip. The phrasing is deliberately deadpan, adding to the absurdity.

The structure here is classic misdirection: build an image (host as trend-savvy), then pull the rug out (host is clueless and reliant on researchers). It’s also a bit of a meta-joke — referencing the production process of the show itself, where hosts are often briefed on guests they might not know personally.

**How it Lands**  
For the Never Mind the Buzzcocks audience — who are used to ironic, self-aware, and often cutting humor — this joke fits right in. It pokes fun at the artifice of showbiz knowledge while also taking a gentle dig at the audience. There’s a touch of snobbery, but it’s immediately undercut by the host’s ignorance, making it harmless and funny.

The joke also works because of the tone of delivery — typically dry and sardonic — and the fact that Tahita Bulmer, while “cool,” may not be mainstream-famous, making the joke’s premise feel accurate. The audience enjoys being in on the joke that the host doesn’t actually know who she is.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Home Audience: 3 — An audible snort. The joke is clever and self-aware, but it doesn’t hit you over the head. It’s more of a “ha, fair enough” moment.
- Comedy Writer: 4 — LOL. Structurally sound, layered with irony, and well-calibrated for tone. A solid example of how to write a dry, self-deprecating intro.

# Keith Chegwin's Intro

> “Also on Noel's team is one of Britain's most loved early morning impromptu doorstep cash prize givers. It's recovering TV presenter, Keith Chegwin.”

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction relies on affectionate mockery and a tongue-in-cheek treatment of British television culture.

- “One of Britain's most loved early morning impromptu doorstep cash prize givers” refers to Chegwin’s well-known role on shows like The Big Breakfast and GMTV, where he surprised people at home with cash prizes. The phrasing is purposefully long and pompous, exaggerating a somewhat kitschy and low-brow job, making it sound almost noble or mythic.

- “Recovering TV presenter” is the punchline. It’s a play on “recovering alcoholic,” “recovering addict,” etc., applied here to Chegwin’s status as a faded or retired TV personality. It’s gently mocking — suggesting that being a TV presenter is something one has to ‘recover’ from, like a vice or illness.

The humor lies in the contrast between Chegwin’s wholesome on-screen persona and this tongue-in-cheek suggestion that he’s in some form of rehab for being a TV presenter.

**How it Lands**  
This joke works particularly well for British audiences familiar with Chegwin’s career, especially his energetic, somewhat goofy persona. The line “recovering TV presenter” gets a solid laugh because it’s unexpected and slightly cruel, but not mean-spirited.

It also taps into a broader cultural awareness — the idea of washed-up celebrities, and the way nostalgia can turn into mockery. It’s funny because there’s truth in it, but it’s delivered with enough affection that it avoids seeming like a cheap shot.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Home Audience: 4 — LOL. The specificity of “doorstep cash prize givers” followed by the dry “recovering TV presenter” line hits a sweet spot of nostalgia and sarcasm.
- Comedy Writer: 4 — LOL. Excellent use of phrasing and a tight punchline. It’s a well-constructed mini-character sketch in a single sentence.

# Dappy's Intro

> “Phil's first guest is from urban collective N-Dubz. This year he won a MOBO, which you may say is worthless. However, it does entitle him to a mammoth 20 nectar points and free entry to the Keswick Pencil Museum. It's Dappy, ladies and gentlemen.”

**Intended Humour**  
This is the most layered and absurdist intro in the batch, and it contains multiple jokes.

- “Urban collective N-Dubz” is a slightly over-formal way of describing a pop/grime group, already hinting at a comedic tone.

- “This year he won a MOBO, which you may say is worthless.” This line sets up a sarcastic dig at the MOBOs (Music of Black Origin Awards), implying they lack prestige. It’s intentionally provocative — the host isn’t saying it, but attributing it to “you,” the audience, creating plausible deniability.

- “However, it does entitle him to a mammoth 20 nectar points and free entry to the Keswick Pencil Museum.” This is pure absurdism. The joke is that the MOBO award comes with hilariously low-stakes perks — 20 nectar points (the UK’s supermarket loyalty scheme, worth about 10p), and free entry to the Keswick Pencil Museum (a famously mundane attraction). These “rewards” are intentionally anticlimactic and absurdly trivial, undercutting the significance of winning an award.

The rhythm is crucial: setup (MOBO win) → undercut (worthless) → absurd payoff (pencil museum). It mirrors a classic three-beat structure, with the third beat being the silliest.

**How it Lands**  
This is a textbook example of Buzzcocks humor — irreverent, slightly mean, but delightfully surreal. It pokes fun at celebrity culture and the inflation of achievements while using very British references (Nectar points, the Keswick Pencil Museum) to maximize relatability and silliness.

For the home audience, this lands really well — especially if they’re familiar with Dappy’s divisive public image. The joke doesn’t attack Dappy directly so much as ridicule the idea of awards as status symbols, which fits the show’s anti-establishment tone.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Home Audience: 5 — ROFL. The specificity of the rewards and the suggestion that a MOBO is only worth 20 Nectar points is so ridiculous it’s hilarious.
- Comedy Writer: 5 — ROFL. Brilliant escalation, clever use of British cultural references, and structurally perfect. It’s both a character roast and a surreal punchline in one.

# Rich Fulcher's Intro

> “And his second guest is the mighty Bruce Star and friend of Noel Fielding, but that's not why he's here. It's quiz show asset and valid booking, Rich Fulcher.”

**Intended Humour**  
This one is subtler, using awkward phrasing and deliberate understatements for comedic effect.

- “The mighty Bruce Star and friend of Noel Fielding” is an odd phrasing choice. “Bruce Star” isn’t a known title or term, so it’s likely a mangled or made-up phrase, possibly meant to sound like something grand but ultimately meaningless. Alternatively, it could be a mishearing or inside joke. If “Bruce Star” is meant to refer to a role Fulcher played (for instance in The Mighty Boosh), it’s intentionally oblique.

- “But that’s not why he’s here” undercuts the previous line — suggesting that being Noel’s friend or a performer of note isn’t a valid reason to appear on the show. This transitions into the final punchline:

- “Quiz show asset and valid booking” is the dryest part, functioning as deadpan humor. Referring to someone as a “valid booking” is hilariously bureaucratic — as if the producers had to justify that he’s not just some weirdo friend of Noel’s, but a “legitimate” guest. It’s a parody of formal language applied to something inherently informal and silly.

**How it Lands**  
This is a bit niche. For viewers familiar with Rich Fulcher’s work in The Mighty Boosh and other shows, the joke lands as a knowing nod. The humor lies in the awkwardness and understatement — calling someone a “quiz show asset” feels like something you’d read on a spreadsheet, not a compliment.

That said, it’s not as laugh-out-loud funny as the Dappy intro. It’s more wry and ironic, and might go over the heads of viewers unfamiliar with Fulcher’s reputation or the behind-the-scenes nature of panel show bookings.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Home Audience: 2 — A minor exhale out the nose. Amusing, but not gut-busting. It depends on knowing Fulcher and enjoying awkward phrasing.
- Comedy Writer: 3 — An audible snort. It’s clever and dry, and the phrase “valid booking” is a gem in industry in-jokes.

—

Final Thoughts  
These introductions expertly blend character comedy, absurdism, and insider wit. The humor ranges from gentle ribbing (Chegwin) to sharp satire (Dappy), all tied together by the show’s signature tone: irreverent, self-aware, and British to its bones.

“On Phil's team tonight... it's award-winning singer-songwriter KT Tunstall.”

**How it Lands**  
This functions more as a setup than a comedic moment. The lack of a joke here makes sense in the flow of the segment, as it serves to ground the audience before the tone shifts into more absurd or sarcastic territory with the next guest introductions. # Lauren Laverne's Intro

“Phil's second guest presents the Culture Show and recently became a mother. Ooh, isn't she highbrow and fertile! What's she going to do next, give birth to the Venus de Milo whilst wearing glasses? It's Lauren Laverne.”

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction contains multiple layers of humor, relying on juxtaposition, absurdist imagery, and satirical exaggeration.

1. “Presents the Culture Show and recently became a mother.” – This sets up two descriptors: one intellectual (“Culture Show”) and one biological/personal (“became a mother”). On their own, these are neutral facts.

2. “Ooh, isn't she highbrow and fertile!” – This line is the first explicit joke. The humor arises from the incongruous combination of “highbrow” (a term associated with intellectualism) and “fertile” (typically a biological descriptor with more primal or domestic connotations). The juxtaposition is intentionally jarring and ridiculous, suggesting that these two unrelated traits somehow elevate Lauren Laverne’s status in a surreal way.

3. “What’s she going to do next, give birth to the Venus de Milo whilst wearing glasses?” – This escalates the surrealism. The Venus de Milo is a famous classical sculpture, symbolizing high art and aesthetic perfection. Giving birth to it is a ludicrous proposition, and the addition of “whilst wearing glasses” adds another layer of intellectual stereotype—glasses being a visual shorthand for being “bookish” or “clever.” The imagery is absurd and intentionally over-the-top, amplifying the earlier juxtaposition between high culture and fertility.

The joke works by taking two relatively mundane facts and extrapolating them to a ludicrous extreme. The comedy lies in the absurdity of the imagined scenario, with an undercurrent of gentle mockery of the way women in media are often praised for “having it all” or being both “smart and maternal.” It also pokes fun at the BBC’s penchant for intellectual programming.

**How it Lands**  
For the typical Never Mind the Buzzcocks audience—UK viewers familiar with pop culture and BBC programming—this joke is likely to be well-received. Lauren Laverne is a known figure who bridges the worlds of indie music and cultural commentary, so the exaggerated praise feels both affectionate and cheekily irreverent. The use of absurdist imagery is consistent with the show’s tone, which often takes mundane facts about guests and spirals them into surreal or sarcastic inventions.

Comedy writers would appreciate the escalation and specificity of the joke. The “Venus de Milo whilst wearing glasses” line is particularly well-crafted, combining classical reference and nerd stereotype into one punchy image.

**Funniness Ratings**  
Audience at home: 4 – LOL  
Comedy writer: 5 – ROFL

# Reverend Jon McClure's Intro

“Bill's first guest is from Sheffield indie electro funk legends Reverend and the Makers. While some pop shows will content themselves with just any old 'Maker', we've only gone and got the actual 'Reverend'! It's actually Reverend Jon McClure!”

**Intended Humour**  
This is a pun-based joke with a touch of mock grandeur. The humor hinges on the band’s name, “Reverend and the Makers,” which is a plural group identity. The joke plays on this structure by pretending that other shows might settle for having just a “Maker” (i.e., any other member of the band), but this show has done one better: they’ve secured “the actual Reverend,” the frontman, Jon McClure.

There’s a mock-serious tone to the phrasing: “we’ve only gone and got the actual ‘Reverend’!” amplifies the sense of accomplishment in a deliberately melodramatic way, as if this is a rare and prestigious get. The repetition of “actual” adds to the faux excitement, mimicking tabloid or PR language.

It’s essentially a wordplay joke, where the band’s name is reinterpreted literally. “Reverend” becomes not just a stage name but a character of singular importance.

**How it Lands**  
This joke is lightweight but well-crafted. For viewers familiar with the band, the joke lands smoothly—it’s a clever twist on a known name. For those unfamiliar, the humor may pass them by, but the phrasing is exaggerated enough to convey that it’s meant to be funny. The mock-celebratory tone is part of the show’s house style: self-aware, slightly camp, and never too earnest.

Comedy writers may see this as a textbook example of a safe, pun-based intro. It’s not groundbreaking, but it’s neat and structurally sound. It plays with language and avoids being mean-spirited.

**Funniness Ratings**

# Jeremy Edwards' Intro

“And finally, it's former Hollyoaks, Holby City, and Rachel Stevens' hunk, now concentrating on solo hunk projects. It's the Incredible Hunk, Jeremy Edwards!”

**Intended Humour**  
This joke has several components: celebrity name-dropping, meta commentary on fame, and a pun.

1. “Former Hollyoaks, Holby City, and Rachel Stevens’ hunk” – This is humorous in its phrasing. By including “Rachel Stevens’ hunk,” the narrator makes it sound as though being someone’s romantic partner is a professional credit. This is a sly jab at how celebrity status can be inflated by associations rather than accomplishments.

2. “Now concentrating on solo hunk projects” – This is a satirical line. The phrase “solo projects” is typically used for musicians branching out from bands. Here, it’s applied to the idea of being a “hunk,” suggesting that Jeremy Edwards is now focusing solely on being attractive, as though that’s a career in itself. The absurdity lies in the idea of “hunk” as both a title and a creative endeavor.

3. “The Incredible Hunk” – This is the punchline: a pun on “The Incredible Hulk,” the well-known Marvel superhero. By replacing “Hulk” with “Hunk,” the joke creates a humorous image of Jeremy Edwards as a ripped, superhuman sex symbol. It’s light, silly, and leans into the playful objectification that the intro has been building toward.

**How it Lands**  
The joke is effective because it’s multilayered: it teases the conventions of celebrity intros, riffs on the shallow media focus on appearance, and ends with a satisfying pun. For Never Mind the Buzzcocks’ audience—used to cheeky irreverence and pop culture references—this is right in the show’s wheelhouse.

Comedy writers would appreciate the rhythm and escalation of the joke. It starts with a list of credentials that gradually become more absurd, culminating in a pun that ties it all together. It’s a classic structure: set-up, exaggeration, punchline.

**Funniness Ratings**  
Audience at home: 4 – LOL  
Comedy writer: 4 – LOL

# Irwin Sparkes's intro
“Bill's first guest is lead singer with the chart-topping Hoosiers. You may know them from their in no way annoying TV advert which certainly didn't make me want to drill into my own brain and suck out the memories. It's Irwin Sparkes!”

**Intended Humour**

This introduction clearly contains a joke. The humor is rooted in sarcasm and hyperbole aimed at mocking a supposedly irritating TV advert featuring The Hoosiers’ music. The line “in no way annoying TV advert” is sarcastic—deliberately stating the opposite of what the speaker actually means. This is followed by an exaggerated claim: “which certainly didn’t make me want to drill into my own brain and suck out the memories.” This is a form of dark hyperbole, escalating a minor annoyance (a catchy or overplayed advert jingle) to a grotesque and absurd mental image of self-inflicted lobotomy, played for comedic effect.

The joke is twofold:
1. Sarcastic praise: The use of faux-positivity to imply the opposite—i.e., the advert was extremely irritating.
2. Extreme exaggeration: The over-the-top violent imagery creates a jarring contrast with the relatively mundane annoyance of a repetitive advert, enhancing the comedic absurdity.

**How it Lands**

This sort of humor is very much in line with Never Mind the Buzzcocks’ tone—irreverent, sarcastic, and laced with dark or absurdist humor. The show's audience, likely familiar with British pop culture and adverts, would understand and appreciate the sarcasm, especially if they too found the advert annoying. The joke works particularly well if viewers themselves were overexposed to the jingle, creating a shared cultural annoyance.

The hyperbolic imagery adds a layer of visceral comedy, appealing to those who enjoy darker humor. However, since it relies on the audience knowing the ad, its effectiveness may dip for viewers unfamiliar with it.

**Funniness Ratings**

- Home Audience: 4 (LOL) — For those who recognize the advert, the sarcastic exaggeration is highly relatable and funny.
- Comedy Writer: 3 (An audible snort) — It’s a solid, well-crafted joke, but structurally familiar and not especially innovative.

# Kevin Eldon's intro
“Bill's second guest is from critically acclaimed comedies such as Nighty Night, I'm Alan Partridge and who could forget BBC2's Hyperdrive. Nope? 9ish? Thursdays at that woman... Okay, it's Kevin Eldon ladies and gentlemen!”

**Intended Humour**

This introduction also contains multiple jokes, primarily structured around faux-forgetfulness and diminishing enthusiasm. The speaker starts with a list of notable comedies, implying increasing obscurity, culminating in BBC2’s Hyperdrive. The phrase “who could forget” is ironic—because the immediate follow-up is, in fact, “Nope?”, suggesting that the speaker (and by extension the audience) has already forgotten it.

The joke escalates as the speaker pretends to struggle to remember when and where Hyperdrive aired (“9ish? Thursdays?”), and even scrambles over the name of the actress in it (“at that woman…”). This comedic device—feigned forgetfulness—plays on the idea of a show that failed to make a cultural impact, despite being “critically acclaimed.”

The final punchline—"Okay, it's Kevin Eldon ladies and gentlemen!"—functions as a comedic release, made funnier by the preceding confusion and anti-climax.

**How it Lands**

This is classic British panel show humor: self-aware, a bit mean-spirited, and poking fun at both the guest and the BBC's more obscure programming. Kevin Eldon, a cult comedy favorite, is known for his offbeat roles, so the joke fits well with his persona.

The audience, likely familiar with the shows mentioned (especially Alan Partridge), would enjoy the meta-humor around Hyperdrive’s obscurity. The intentionally botched delivery adds to the charm, simulating a presenter running out of steam mid-introduction.

**Funniness Ratings**

- Home Audience: 4 (LOL) — The layered irony and deliberately poor memory are funny, especially for comedy fans.
- Comedy Writer: 4 (LOL) — The structure is clever, and the timing of the delivery provides a good payoff. It plays with expectations in an intelligent way.

# Patrick Wolf's intro
“On Phil's team is the visionary, left-field, avant-garde, genre-defying, classically trained singer-songwriter, and he recently called Mika a twat. It's Patrick Wolf!”

**Intended Humour**

This joke relies on the juxtaposition of highbrow descriptors with a sudden, crude punchline. The introduction starts with a serious, almost pretentious buildup: “visionary,” “left-field,” “avant-garde,” “genre-defying,” “classically trained.” These descriptors set up a tone of reverence, painting Patrick Wolf as an artistic genius.

Then, the line takes a sharp swerve into the vulgar with “and he recently called Mika a twat.” The shift from lofty praise to playground insult is the crux of the humor. It’s a bait-and-switch—the audience is led to expect a dignified finish, and instead gets a comic jolt via profanity.

This kind of tonal whiplash is a frequent comedic technique on Buzzcocks: building a formal or serious structure only to puncture it with something crass or absurd.

**How it Lands**

This joke is likely to land well. The contrast between the high-art language and the low-brow insult is stark and surprising, which is often a key ingredient in comedy. The audience probably knows who Mika is, and the idea of an avant-garde artist being so blunt and petty is inherently funny.

The word “twat” is also one of those British swear words that still retains comedic value due to its sound and delivery. It’s less offensive than some stronger expletives but still edgy enough to get a laugh.

**Funniness Ratings**

- Home Audience: 4 (LOL) — The delivery and tonal shift are effective and likely to provoke laughter.
- Comedy Writer: 4 (LOL) — Strong structure and well-timed misdirection. Not groundbreaking but very effective.

# Miquita Oliver's intro
“His second guest is the shining light of Channel 4's popular youth strand T4. She's charmed everyone from Robert De Niro to 50 Cent and she recently called Mika a *bleep* riddled *bleep* basket of a *bleep*! It's my good friend and colleague, Miquita Oliver.”

**Intended Humour**

This is another joke built on a familiar comedic structure: the contrast between dignified buildup and shocking vulgarity. It mirrors Patrick Wolf’s intro but turns the knob up several notches.

First, we get the respectable setup: “shining light,” “popular youth strand,” and name-dropping big celebrities like Robert De Niro and 50 Cent. This sets up an image of a polished, well-liked media personality.

Then comes the punchline: “she recently called Mika a *bleep* riddled *bleep* basket of a *bleep*!” The humor here lies in the absurd escalation of profanity, entirely bleeped out, which leaves the exact insult to the audience’s imagination.

The bleeping itself is part of the joke—it suggests something too offensive to air, which paradoxically makes it funnier. The more bleeps, the more outrageous the imagined insult becomes. It’s a kind of meta-humor, where the censorship is the punchline.

Also, the fact that this is the second joke in a row involving someone insulting Mika creates a running gag. The repetition adds a layer of satire, implying that everyone in the industry has a vendetta against Mika, which is funny in its own right.

**How it Lands**

This joke leans heavily on shock value and absurdity. The sheer quantity of bleeps is unexpected and builds anticipation. The audience’s imagination fills in the blanks, often with something even more profane than what was actually said.

The added comedy comes from the fact that Miquita Oliver is being introduced as a professional presenter, yet is being quoted as delivering a wildly offensive insult. The contradiction between persona and language boosts the humor.

The running gag about Mika being insulted also gives it an extra comedic layer, suggesting an ongoing joke or vendetta.

**Funniness Ratings**

- Home Audience: 5 (ROFL) — The sheer audacity and timing of the bleeps make this a standout laugh moment.
- Comedy Writer: 4 (LOL) — It’s a strong joke structurally, with great escalation and callback, though reliant on a well-worn shock tactic.

—

In conclusion, all four introductions contain jokes structured around misdirection, sarcasm, vulgarity, and irony—hallmarks of Never Mind the Buzzcocks' comedic style. The segment is particularly effective due to its balance of intellectual and lowbrow humor, and its layered callbacks (especially targeting Mika) add coherence and comedic payoff.

# Ryan Jarman's intro

> “And his first guest is number four on the NME Cool List making him officially even cooler than Craig Finn, Eddie Argos, or Yannis Philippakis, it’s Ryan Jarman from The Cribs!”

**Intended Humour**

This introduction contains a single, layered joke based on the absurdity and superficiality of media-generated lists like the “NME Cool List.” The humor is drawn from the mock-serious tone in which the announcer treats the idea that being #4 on the list “officially” makes someone "cooler" than specific named musicians.

Here’s how the joke works:

- The phrase “officially even cooler than…” plays on the mock authority of the NME's Cool List, as if it's a scientific or governmental designation rather than a subjective, often arbitrary editorial piece.
- Listing specific, relatively well-known indie musicians (Craig Finn of The Hold Steady, Eddie Argos of Art Brut, and Yannis Philippakis of Foals) serves to both anchor the joke in musical reality and highlight the absurdity of ranking “coolness” among a niche group.
- The deadpan delivery and faux gravity of “officially even cooler than…” amplifies the satire.

The joke is funny because it pointlessly elevates something inherently trivial, treating the Cool List as if it were a Nobel Prize. This absurdity is a classic British comedic move—mocking pretension and cultural self-importance.

**How it Lands**

- For the show’s typical audience: Buzzcocks viewers tend to be indie music fans or at least pop culture literate; they likely know the NME Cool List and the musicians referenced. So, the comparison resonates, and the audience laughs at the self-aware ridiculousness of such rankings.
- For comedy writers: They’d appreciate the economy of the joke—it skewers both the music industry’s obsession with image and the media’s compulsion to quantify the unquantifiable (coolness). It’s a tight, clever line with a good rhythm and payoff.

**Funniness Ratings**

- Audience at home: 4 (LOL) — It’s a smart, character-based joke that connects with the target demographic.
- Comedy writer: 4 (LOL) — Well-constructed, satirical, and concise.

# David Cross's intro

> “Also on Noel’s team is the Emmy award-winning US star of Arrested Development, coming to our country sitting on our panel shows, Joe Pasquale could have had that seat but it's David Cross!”

**Intended Humour**

This intro contains a joke built around contrast and subversion of expectations.

- It sets up David Cross as an impressive international guest—“Emmy award-winning US star of Arrested Development”—which is already notable for a UK panel show.
- Then it undercuts this prestige with “coming to our country sitting on our panel shows,” a phrase that humorously alludes to the trope of American celebrities slumming it on British media, playing into a kind of cultural inferiority complex.
- The punchline is framed as a bait-and-switch: "Joe Pasquale could have had that seat but it's David Cross!" Joe Pasquale is a UK comedian often associated with lowbrow humor, dated pantomime performances, and a squeaky voice—he is the comic antithesis of David Cross, who is known for edgy, cerebral, often acerbic comedy.

The joke works because it leverages the disparity in comedic styles and reputation between Pasquale and Cross. The idea that Pasquale “could have had that seat” is both absurd (no one would think to interchange them) and mockingly nationalistic: we gave up our own (Pasquale) for this esteemed import. The incongruity is the core of the humor.

**How it Lands**

- For the audience: There’s a good chance they find the joke funny because of the contrast and the absurdity of even comparing David Cross and Joe Pasquale. Pasquale is a familiar punching bag for British comedy fans, so invoking him is an easy laugh.
- For comedy writers: They’ll appreciate the layered structure—first building up Cross, then swerving into lowbrow territory. It’s a tight juxtaposition that delivers a clean payoff through contrast.

**Funniness Ratings**

- Audience at home: 4 (LOL) — The unexpected shift to Joe Pasquale as the “alternative” gets an audible laugh.
- Comedy writer: 4 (LOL) — Strong juxtaposition and effective use of British comedic archetypes.

# Joel Pott's intro  
"On Bill's team tonight, the lead singer from one of those Snow Patrol-style bands, it's Joel Pott from Athlete!"  

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction contains one clear joke, which relies on a blend of musical stereotyping and dismissive familiarity. The humour comes from the phrase “one of those Snow Patrol-style bands,” which is intentionally vague and dismissive. Snow Patrol is known for their blandly emotional, indie-rock sound—often considered interchangeable with other similar bands from the early 2000s. The suggestion that Athlete is just another indistinct band in this genre is the core of the joke.  

The comedic mechanism here is mockery through generalization. It implies that Athlete is not artistically distinctive, and their music is so similar to bands like Snow Patrol that there's no point in identifying them directly. That setup undercuts Joel Pott’s significance as a guest, which is a common comedic device on Never Mind the Buzzcocks—mocking or downplaying the celebrity status of guests right from the beginning.  

**How it Lands**  
For the show's typical audience—UK viewers who are musically literate and familiar with the indie scene of the early 2000s—this joke would likely land well. The audience is expected to know that Athlete and Snow Patrol were often grouped together in the post-Britpop, guitar-driven, emotionally earnest genre. The dismissiveness is part of the show's charm—Buzzcocks thrives on belittling its guests in a tongue-in-cheek way.  

To a home viewer, especially anyone who remembers that era of music, the joke hits as a clever jab, though perhaps not laugh-out-loud funny. To a comedy writer, the joke is structurally sound but relies heavily on shared cultural knowledge. It’s efficient and dry, more smirk-worthy than uproarious.

# Kimberley Stewart's intro  
"Also on Bill's team, not just Rod Stewart's daughter, she's also in her own right a friend of Paris Hilton, it's Kimberley Stewart!"  

**Intended Humour**  
Yes, this contains a joke, and a fairly layered one. The humour here is built around faux-flattery and sarcastic diminishing of Kimberley Stewart’s personal achievements. First, the intro acknowledges her as “Rod Stewart’s daughter,” indicating her fame is inherited. Then it adds, “she’s also in her own right a friend of Paris Hilton,” which is the punchline.  

The joke hinges on the absurdity of framing being Paris Hilton’s friend as an independent accomplishment. It plays on the idea that Kimberley, despite being famous, might not have done anything particularly noteworthy herself—and that her most significant personal claim to fame is a social connection to another celebrity known for being famous for being famous.  

In comedic terms, this is a classic example of ironic praise: it sounds like it’s building her up, but it’s actually undercutting her credibility by implying that her fame is totally unearned, even twice removed.  

**How it Lands**  
For the Buzzcocks audience, who are well-versed in celebrity culture and the tabloid scene of the early 2000s, this punchline is likely to draw a good laugh. At the time, Paris Hilton was the poster child for vacuous celebrity, and saying Kimberley is “a friend of Paris Hilton” is essentially saying, “she’s famous for nothing.”  

This is classic Buzzcocks snark—undermining celebrity guests with cutting sarcasm. People watching at home would likely appreciate the sharpness, while comedy writers would admire the economy of the punchline, though it’s not especially innovative.

# Lethal Bizzle's intro

**How it Lands**

For comedy writers, it's a nice mix of parody, rhythm, and tonal misdirection. It shows good understanding of comedic timing and the use of repetition for humour. The fact that the host mimics the chant three times before delivering the punchline shows confidence in its rhythm and payoff.

# Preston's Intro

> “Preston, Bill's first guest is Ordinary Boy and professional celebrity. Preston appeared on ‘Celebrity Big Brother’ and then married the winner, Chantelle. Preston was worried it would alienate hardcore Ordinary Boys fans, but luckily, neither of them were in the country, or existed.”

---

**Intended Humour**

This intro contains two layered jokes:

1. **“Ordinary Boy and professional celebrity.”**
   - This is a subtle jab. “Ordinary Boy” refers both to Preston’s band (The Ordinary Boys) and becomes a descriptor mocking his lack of distinction—he’s just an “ordinary” person. Calling him a “professional celebrity” pokes fun at his fame stemming not from musical talent, but from celebrity culture and reality TV. The phrase implies he’s better known for being famous than for anything he’s done.
   - This is a satire of the mid-2000s boom in “famous for being famous” reality stars, a demographic that Preston epitomized at the time.

2. **“...alienate hardcore Ordinary Boys fans, but luckily, neither of them were in the country, or existed.”**
   - This is classic British sarcasm. It plays on the idea that Preston was concerned about alienating fans by marrying Chantelle (a reality star), but the punchline undercuts his concern by suggesting he doesn’t have any real fans to begin with.
   - The joke escalates from “neither of them were in the country” (already suggesting a very small fanbase) to “or existed,” which annihilates the notion entirely.
   - The humor lies in deflating Preston’s self-importance and mocking the perceived lack of legitimacy of his band’s fanbase.

---

**How it Lands**

This joke would land quite well with the show's target audience—UK viewers familiar with pop culture, especially reality TV and British indie bands of the mid-2000s. Preston’s rise to fame was widely mocked, and this intro capitalizes on that. The humor is both clever and cutting.

- The line about “neither of them existed” is especially sharp and would likely get a big response from the studio audience.
- For home viewers, it plays into the show’s well-known tradition of taking the piss out of pop stars, particularly those perceived as overhyped or lacking talent.

“Phil's first guest is former Spice Girl and retired sporting legend, it's Melanie Chisholm. Melanie once branded Cliff Richard's Millennium Prayer a complete pile of shit.”

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction contains a joke, primarily in the juxtaposition and surprise of the last sentence. The humor arises from the contrast between Melanie Chisholm’s public image (as a pop star and sportswoman) and the unexpectedly blunt, profane language used in the quote about Cliff Richard's song. 

Let’s break it down:

- “Former Spice Girl and retired sporting legend” is both accurate and mildly playful. She was "Sporty Spice" and is known for her athleticism. Calling her a “retired sporting legend” is a comic exaggeration of her persona, especially if taken too literally, as if she were a former Olympian.
- The punchline comes from the abrupt shift to her quote about Cliff Richard’s “Millennium Prayer,” a famously earnest and somewhat divisive song. The phrase “a complete pile of shit” is funny because:
  - It’s direct and unexpectedly vulgar.
  - It’s a jarring contrast to the reverent tone typically surrounding Cliff Richard, a national institution in the UK.
  - It plays into the Buzzcocks tradition of irreverence and celebrity mockery.

**How it Lands**  
For the audience, this joke is likely to land well. Buzzcocks viewers expect and enjoy cheeky, cutting comments about pop culture figures. Melanie’s quote fits that mold perfectly. Her surprising bluntness is part of the humor—it humanizes her and gives the audience an insider-y thrill of hearing a celebrity go “off-script.”

For comedy writers, this is a solid, well-constructed intro. The first half sets up a conventional profile of a pop star, and the punchline delivers an unexpected twist. There’s economy and rhythm: the intro is short, the reveal is sharp, and the quote is gloriously indelicate.

# Noel Fielding's Intro

"Bill's first guest is Mighty Boosh comedy man and lovely-haired cool dude, Noel Fielding. Noel hates traditional jokes, which is a shame because I was talking to an Englishman, Irishman, and Scotsman the other day, and something particularly funny happened, but you'll never find out, Fielding."

---

**Intended Humour**

This introduction contains a clear joke, structured around a meta-comedic setup and subversion of expectations.

The humor here revolves around the well-worn structure of a classic joke: “An Englishman, an Irishman, and a Scotsman walk into a bar…” This type of joke is an old-fashioned setup often used in British comedy, and its format is well-known to the audience — it's immediately recognizable as the opening to a joke that will play on national stereotypes.

The punchline, though, is a deliberate anti-joke. The host (likely Simon Amstell, given the era) sets up the classic joke structure to bait the audience into anticipating a punchline, only to deny it: “...but you’ll never find out, Fielding.”

The joke is twofold:

1. It mocks traditional joke structures, aligning with Noel Fielding’s own surreal, alternative comedy style (as stated: "Noel hates traditional jokes"). The refusal to tell the punchline is a wink to Fielding’s comedic preferences — it’s a “joke” made in the shape of a traditional joke, but then yanked away in a sort of comedic protest.

2. The punchline is also a gentle jab at Fielding — “you’ll never find out, Fielding” — presenting the denial of the payoff as a petty act of revenge or playful punishment for Fielding's disdain of classic joke formats.

This meta-humor (a joke about jokes) is typical of Never Mind the Buzzcocks’ style, especially during Amstell’s tenure, which was marked by clever, caustic, and often self-referential humor.

---

**How it Lands**

For the audience at home — especially fans of British comedy — the setup and subversion are likely to land well. The classic joke format is universally known among UK viewers, and the twist is unexpected and amusing, particularly when it's tailored to Noel Fielding’s well-established comedic persona. The audience enjoys a moment of shared recognition: we all know how these jokes go — but this time, we’re in on the joke that there’s no joke.

For comedy writers, the joke is clever in its construction. It acknowledges the audience’s expectations, builds them up, and then flips them. It also ties in character humor effectively: the joke isn’t just random, it’s targeted at a guest’s personality and comedic ethos. That makes it smart and layered — a joke about jokes, a joke about Fielding, and a joke about refusing to joke.

---

**Funniness Ratings**

- Home Audience: 4 (LOL)
- Comedy Writer: 4 (LOL)

# Alfie Jackson's Intro

"His second guest is the Holloways' hip indie frontman, Alfie Jackson. Like the Libertines, the Holloways have two frontmen, but unlike Carl and Pete, Alfie and Rob don't have a highly charged erotic frisson on stage. They just have wonderful sex."

---

**Intended Humour**

This introduction contains a joke that hinges on escalation and misdirection.

It begins with a seemingly dry music fact: that the Holloways, like the Libertines, have two frontmen. This sets up an expectation of a standard comparison between indie bands. The reference to the Libertines’ Carl Barât and Pete Doherty’s “highly charged erotic frisson” is a nod to the intense, sometimes homoerotic stage chemistry the two famously shared — a well-known piece of rock mythology.

The punchline twists this with the abrupt, deadpan claim: "They just have wonderful sex." The joke works by escalating from implicit to explicit. Where the Libertines' chemistry is described in flirtatious, artsy terms (“erotic frisson”), the Holloways are bluntly said to be sexually active with each other.

There are multiple layers of comedy:

- Misdirection: The audience expects a tame observation about musical dynamics. Instead, they get a graphic and absurdly casual declaration about bandmates having sex.

- Incongruity: The contrast between highbrow phrasing (“erotic frisson”) and the bluntness of “wonderful sex” is jarring and funny.

- Satire: It pokes fun at the tendency of music journalism to romanticize male friendships in bands, almost fetishizing them.

- Deadpan delivery: The line is delivered as if it's just another piece of trivia, which makes it even funnier.

---

**How it Lands**

For the audience at home, this joke is likely to get a strong response. It’s unexpected, slightly risqué, and taps into a common pop culture trope (the over-analysis of male musicians’ friendships). It’s also framed as a punchline — the rhythm and timing are strong.

For comedy writers, the joke is elegant in structure: it walks the audience down a garden path and then opens a trap door. The escalation from subtext (Libertines) to text (Holloways) is cleanly executed. It’s also character-focused (Alfie), making it relevant rather than random.

---

**Funniness Ratings**

- Home Audience: 5 (ROFL)
- Comedy Writer: 4 (LOL)

# Donny Tourette's Intro

"Phil's first guest is tower of London punk frontman and reality punk TV star, Donny Tourette. Poignantly echoing punk legend Sid Vicious's untimely death from a heroin overdose, Donny Tourette once got thrown off a London bus after refusing to buy a ticket. And to think, the Sex Pistols only sang about anarchy in the UK. This guy's living it, man!"

---

**Intended Humour**

This introduction contains layered satire and is structured as a joke.

The humor relies heavily on contrast and hyperbolic comparison. It sets up a dramatic parallel between Sid Vicious — a punk legend who died tragically from a heroin overdose — and Donny Tourette, who is mocked for a far less grandiose act of rebellion: getting kicked off a bus for not paying his fare.

The joke's construction is classic satire:

1. The setup is solemn and reverent — “Poignantly echoing … untimely death from a heroin overdose” — suggesting a grand, tragic tale of punk martyrdom.

2. The punchline undercuts it completely — “once got thrown off a London bus after refusing to buy a ticket” — an act that is both mundane and petty.

3. The follow-up line — “This guy’s living it, man!” — is dripping with sarcastic approval. It parodies over-the-top tributes to punk authenticity by praising something that’s entirely unimpressive.

Comedically, the joke functions as a take-down of celebrity self-mythologizing and shallow rebellion. It reduces Donny’s “anarchy” to a minor fare-dodging incident, poking fun at how reality TV figures often posture as rebellious without the substance.

---

**How it Lands**

The home audience is likely to find this funny, especially those familiar with punk lore and the self-seriousness of some performers. Donny Tourette is ripe for ridicule: a minor celebrity who cultivates a punk image despite having participated in reality shows — the contrast is inherently comical.

Comedy writers will appreciate the elegance of the misdirection — how the solemnity of Sid Vicious’s tragedy is used to punch down at Donny’s absurdly minor transgression. The layered sarcasm hits hard without being cruel, and the final “This guy’s living it, man!” is a pitch-perfect parody of shallow rebellion.

---

**Funniness Ratings**

- Home Audience: 4 (LOL)

# Philip Olivier's Intro

"Phil's second guest is Hollyoaks in the City star and winner of The Games, Sir Philip Olivier. Philip is known for his toned torso and beefcake physique, but just because he's a big, muscly guy doesn't mean he can't act. That's pure coincidence."

---

**Intended Humour**

This is a classic example of a punchline that plays with audience expectations through ironic reversal.

The joke sets up a defense of Olivier’s acting skills: “Just because he's a big, muscly guy doesn't mean he can't act." This mirrors a common trope — defending someone who doesn’t fit the mold of a “serious actor.” But then it immediately undercuts that defense with “That's pure coincidence,” implying that yes, in fact, he can’t act — it’s not because he’s muscly, he just happens to also be bad.

The humor lies in:

- Irony: The setup promises a progressive defense, only to slam the subject harder than if it had just said he can’t act.

- Timing: The phrase “That’s pure coincidence” is short, sharp, and delivered after a pause, making it an effective punchline.

- Tone: The deadpan delivery makes it sound like a sincere clarification, which enhances the absurdity.

This is a joke at Olivier’s expense, but it’s wrapped in such a tight structure that it feels more clever than cruel.

---

**How it Lands**

The home audience will likely find this funny, especially because it plays into the stereotype of soap opera actors being more about looks than talent. The twist is both surprising and familiar.

Comedy writers will appreciate the economy of language and the sharpness of the reversal. It's a clean, efficient joke that delivers a laugh without needing elaborate setup.

# Intro Text
#----------------------#
Bill's first guest would be the leading comedian of his generation, if it wasn't for that ridiculous hair, it's Alan Davies.  Men want to have a drink with him, women want to sleep with him, and people who've got their mortgages from the Abbey want to punch him in his trustworthy face.

# Test Model Analysis
#----------------------#
# Alan Davies's intro  
“Bill's first guest would be the leading comedian of his generation, if it wasn't for that ridiculous hair, it's Alan Davies. Men want to have a drink with him, women want to sleep with him, and people who've got their mortgages from the Abbey want to punch him in his trustworthy face.”

---

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction contains multiple jokes, each employing a different comedic technique. Let's break them down:

1. “Bill's first guest would be the leading comedian of his generation, if it wasn't for that ridiculous hair…”  
   - This joke is a light roast of Alan Davies, hinging on the comic device of undercutting praise with an absurd or trivial flaw. It begins with a hyperbolic compliment, suggesting Alan Davies could be “the leading comedian of his generation” — a grand, lofty claim — which is then immediately undercut with the phrase “if it wasn't for that ridiculous hair.”  
   - The humor lies in the juxtaposition: the audience is led to expect a genuine compliment, but instead it’s subverted by a petty, superficial criticism. The use of “ridiculous hair” is funny because it trivializes the reason someone might not achieve comedic greatness. It’s also a visual gag — Alan Davies is known for his distinctive, curly, somewhat unkempt hair, which makes the joke land visually for those familiar with him.

2. “Men want to have a drink with him, women want to sleep with him…”  
   - This line plays on a common trope used to describe charismatic male celebrities — the idea that they are universally appealing to both sexes in different ways. It’s a classic setup, often seen in parody or satire to mock the way media hypes up male celebrities.  
   - The humor lies partly in its predictability — it’s a cliché, and audiences recognize it as such.

3. “…and people who've got their mortgages from the Abbey want to punch him in his trustworthy face.”  
   - This is the punchline of the whole intro and arguably the funniest part. It subverts the trope established in the previous sentence by adding an absurd third group with a completely different, unexpected reaction.  
   - The humor works in a few ways:
     - “Trustworthy face”: Alan Davies has often played roles (e.g., Jonathan Creek) where he comes across as affable and trustworthy. This phrase plays on that public image.
     - “Abbey”: Refers to Abbey National, a former British building society/bank known for positioning itself as reliable and relatable. Alan Davies featured in ads for them, becoming the face of the brand.
     - The idea that people with mortgages from the Abbey want to “punch him in his trustworthy face” plays on the discontent of customers toward corporations — especially when the public face of the brand is a celebrity. It’s funny because it imagines a violent backlash against a figure who’s supposed to be reassuring. It’s also a comment on how public goodwill can quickly turn sour when tied to financial woes.

**How it Lands**  
This is a strong intro that layers multiple jokes with escalating absurdity and cleverly blends public perception, celebrity tropes, and consumer frustration. For the Never Mind the Buzzcocks audience — who are generally pop-culture savvy and enjoy sardonic British humor — this type of writing resonates well.

The “ridiculous hair” line is a warm roast — playful and personal. It lands well because audiences are familiar with Davies’s look and persona. The “men want to have a drink…” line is a setup, predictable but necessary for the rhythm and misdirection. The final punchline about Abbey mortgages is the killer line — it takes a predictable structure and flips it unexpectedly, tapping into real-world sentiment with surreal exaggeration.

Fans of the show, who are used to irreverent, slightly vicious humor, will appreciate the layered writing here. Comedy writers would admire the tight structure and the way the final punchline pays off the earlier buildup.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Audience: 4 — LOL. The roast is well-pitched, and the final line is a strong, unexpected sucker-punch that rewards familiarity with British culture.
- Comedy Writer: 5 — ROFL. The structure is tight, the subversion is clever, and the joke about Abbey is a brilliant blend of pop culture, advertising satire, and social commentary.

---

# Chris Peck's intro  
“His second guest is Boy Kill Boy frontman, Chris Peck. Last year, when [in] Ibiza, Boy Kill Boy went crazy in their rented mansion, smashing crockery, breaking windows and damaging a bathroom cabinet, causing a massive £10,000 worth of PR credibility.”

---

**Intended Humour**  
This intro contains a joke that hinges on subverted expectations and satire of rockstar behavior.

1. “Last year, when [in] Ibiza, Boy Kill Boy went crazy in their rented mansion…”  
   - This sets up a familiar story trope: the rock band goes wild on holiday. The mention of Ibiza — known for its party culture — primes the audience for predictable tales of debauchery.

2. “Smashing crockery, breaking windows and damaging a bathroom cabinet…”  
   - This list continues to build on the rockstar stereotype, but the choice of destruction is increasingly mundane. “Smashing crockery” and “damaging a bathroom cabinet” are oddly domestic acts of rebellion. This choice makes the band’s supposed mayhem sound petty and unglamorous. It’s a comically underwhelming version of what we expect from a rock group.

3. “Causing a massive £10,000 worth of PR credibility.”  
   - This line is the punchline and the cleverest part of the joke. It subverts the expected consequence — usually one would expect “causing £10,000 worth of damage” — but instead the cost is rephrased as “£10,000 worth of PR credibility.”  
   - The humor hinges on the idea that these antics weren’t damaging at all, but rather enhanced the band's image — a meta-commentary on how chaotic behavior is not only tolerated in the music industry but rewarded as part of image-building.
   - It satirizes the media’s role in turning bad behavior into brand currency: the more outrageous the antics, the better the PR.

**How it Lands**  
This joke works well for the Buzzcocks audience. The show often mocks the music industry’s self-seriousness and hypocrisy, and this joke fits that mold perfectly. The list of petty destruction is funny in its own right, but the twist of the financial impact being “PR credibility” is where the real bite is.

The joke also has a layered critique — it’s not just about Boy Kill Boy but about how bands in general (especially those trying to break through in the mid-2000s indie scene) often rely on contrived stunts to gain media attention. It’s also a bit of a jab at how little it takes to seem “edgy” — breaking a bathroom cabinet becomes a PR win.

To a comedy writer, this joke is particularly satisfying because it uses structure well: it builds a conventional story, adds quirky details to undercut it, then completely inverts the expected ending with a smart satirical punchline. It’s economic in wording but rich in implications.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Audience: 4 — LOL. The misdirection is funny and the critique of music PR culture is sharp. It rewards those who are savvy about how image-making works.
- Comedy Writer: 5 — ROFL. The joke is precise, efficient, and offers layered satire. It takes a tired cliché (rock stars trashing houses) and gives it a sharp, original twist.

---

# Overall Assessment

Both introductions use humor that is characteristically Buzzcocks — irreverent, culturally literate, and slightly cruel in a playful way. The Alan Davies intro is a classic roast with a surprise payoff line that critiques celebrity endorsement culture. The Chris Peck intro is a satirical takedown of indie band PR antics, delivered through subverted expectations and clever wordplay.

Each joke is well-crafted to suit the audience's sensibilities, and both would be appreciated not just by viewers but by those who understand the mechanics of comedy writing.

# Lily Allen's intro

“Phil's first guest is modern pop star and MySpace hussy, Lily Allen. Lily was expelled from five schools. At 14, she ran away to go to Glastonbury and she pushed pills in Ibiza.”

---

**Intended Humour**

This introduction clearly contains multiple jokes, layered across tone, word choice, and subtext.

- “MySpace hussy”: This is the first joke and plays on two things: (1) the cultural moment when MySpace was a significant platform for musicians, especially for self-promotion (which Lily Allen famously used to launch her career), and (2) the use of the word “hussy,” which is intentionally archaic and loaded, suggesting promiscuity or scandalous behavior. The juxtaposition of a modern platform (MySpace) with a 1950s insult creates a humorous dissonance. It's not just calling her a “MySpace user”; it’s calling her a “MySpace hussy,” which is both absurd and cheekily judgmental.

- “Expelled from five schools”: While technically factual, the delivery here functions as a comedic exaggeration. It’s not just that she was expelled once—five times. The comedic implication is that she’s incorrigible or uncontrollable, reinforcing her “bad girl” tabloid persona.

- “She ran away to go to Glastonbury and she pushed pills in Ibiza”: This line combines two references to well-known British party culture landmarks—Glastonbury Festival and Ibiza. “Pushed pills” is slang for selling ecstasy or similar drugs, but in this context, it’s likely an exaggeration or a cheeky reference to her past party lifestyle. The humor lies in the way the sentence stacks up rebellious behaviors (running away, drug-related activity) in a deadpan, list-like fashion—like a résumé of bad behavior. It’s absurd in its casual tone, treating serious infractions like a quirky personality trait.

---

**How it Lands**

- For the show’s audience (largely British viewers familiar with pop culture and tabloids), the jokes land well. Lily Allen was a major figure in British pop at the time, known for her working-class aesthetic, outspokenness, and tabloid notoriety. The MySpace reference dates the joke but would have resonated strongly with contemporary viewers. The “hussy” line is particularly sly, appealing to fans of innuendo and retro slang.

- The list of Lily’s youthful misadventures plays into her public image in a knowingly exaggerated way. The tone is mocking but affectionate—Buzzcocks is known for roasting its guests, and this is an example of that style. The “pushed pills” line skirts the edge of acceptability, which adds an edge of rebellious humor that matches the show's tone.

- Overall, this introduction is sly, character-driven, and plays expertly with British cultural references and tabloid tropes.

# Steve Strange's intro

“His second guest is 80s pop star and party man, Steve Strange. Steve took part in Celebrity Scissorhands for Children In Need. It was an unusual reality show in that it had no winner, but nine losers.”

---

**Intended Humour**

This introduction contains a strong punchline delivered with characteristic Buzzcocks sarcasm.

- The setup is straightforward: Steve Strange is introduced with a nod to his fame and flamboyant image—“80s pop star and party man”—setting the tone for a light roast.

- The first sentence is informational, but the second sentence contains the joke: “It was an unusual reality show in that it had no winner, but nine losers.”

- This is a classic Buzzcocks joke structure: start with a real fact (he appeared on Celebrity Scissorhands), then deliver a punchline that undermines or mocks the subject. The humor comes from the reversal of expectations. Most reality shows crown a winner. Saying there were “no winners, but nine losers” is a cutting way to suggest that everyone involved in the show was terrible, or that the show itself was a failure, or both. It’s a meta-commentary on the low quality or absurdity of certain reality TV formats.

- The deadpan tone of the delivery enhances the joke, making it sound like an observation rather than a punchline, which is typical of the show’s style.

---

**How it Lands**

- For the audience, the joke is quick, snide, and easily understandable. Many viewers would have been aware of Celebrity Scissorhands, which was a bizarre format where celebrities trained to be hairdressers for charity. It was a noble cause but a ridiculous premise, and the joke skewers that without directly insulting the charity aspect.

- The joke would resonate particularly well with viewers who find reality TV ripe for satire. It’s a classic Buzzcocks move—mocking D-list celebrity culture while remaining aware of its entertainment value.

- The humor is sharp but not cruel. Steve Strange’s flamboyant persona makes him a natural target for this kind of affectionate derision.

# Jamelia's intro

It’s funny because “fertile” is not a word typically used to describe celebrities in this context, making it sound more like livestock than a pop star.

- “She almost looked three-quarters of a million dollars”: This is a clever twist on the phrase “a million dollars,” commonly used to describe someone looking stunning. The joke plays on the fact that £400,000 is not quite a million dollars (in conversion), so Jamelia “almost looked three-quarters of a million dollars.” It’s mathematical pedantry applied to a glamor phrase, undercutting the compliment with ironic literalism.

---

**How it Lands**

- The opening description is absurd and campy, and fans of the show will appreciate the cheekiness. The “fertile mother of two” line is especially funny because it’s so unexpected and needlessly specific. It’s a subtle jab at how women in the media are often reduced to their motherhood or sexual appeal—Buzzcocks exaggerates this to highlight the absurdity.

- The follow-up joke is nerdy and clever, appealing to viewers who enjoy wordplay and subverted clichés. The over-precision of “three-quarters of a million dollars” turns a glamorous image into a joke about currency conversion, which is very British in its literal-mindedness and self-deprecation.

- The humor is dry, ironic, and layered, which fits the show’s tone perfectly.

# Danny Jones's intro  
"Bill's first guest is the funky regional McFly hunk, Danny Jones!"  

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction contains one primary joke, built around playful exaggeration and mocking pop star tropes.

The phrase “funky regional McFly hunk” is the comedic centerpiece. It's constructed in a hyperbolic, intentionally silly way to mock the standard way pop stars are often introduced. Let’s break this down:

- “Funky” is a dated and overly enthusiastic adjective commonly used in cheesy pop culture presentations, especially when describing boy band members. It evokes the image of a PR team desperately trying to make someone sound cool.
- “Regional” undercuts “funky” in a very British way. In the UK, calling someone “regional” is a backhanded compliment—it implies they’re not quite national celebrities, and possibly that their appeal is limited to a specific area (e.g., the North of England). This juxtaposition is the main source of humour here; “funky” builds him up, while “regional” brings him right back down.
- “McFly hunk” is a nod to the band McFly’s appeal as a teen heartthrob group. “Hunk” is used ironically—it’s another word that feels lifted from a tabloid or a teenage magazine, and its hammy overuse is part of the joke.

**How it Lands**  
This joke lands fairly well with the show’s audience. Never Mind the Buzzcocks viewers at home—typically music-savvy and culturally literate—are likely to enjoy this kind of affectionate mocking of pop culture clichés. They’d recognize both the formulaic nature of boy-band member introductions and the ironic tone used here.

For comedy writers, the joke is solid if not groundbreaking. It uses classic juxtaposition and undercutting to create a gentle roast. There’s no twist ending or misdirection, but the use of “regional” as a deflationary punchline is a clever flourish.

**Funniness Ratings**  
Audience: 3 – An audible snort  
Comedy writer: 3 – An audible snort  

# Dan Gillespie Sells's intro  
"Phill's first guest is the Feeling frontman and all-round respectable booking, it's Dan Gillespie Sells!"  

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction contains a mild joke, relying on the idea of “respectable booking” being an oddly formal and low-key compliment in the context of a chaotic comedy panel show.

The humour hinges on the phrase “all-round respectable booking.” Here’s the breakdown:

- “The Feeling frontman” is factual and lacks any inherent joke.
- “All-round respectable booking” is where the comedy lives. It’s a deliberately bland, bureaucratic way of praising someone. In a show where intros often lean toward the absurd or mocking, describing a guest as “respectable” is both anti-comedic and subtly sarcastic.
- The joke lies in the incongruity between the wild tone of most intros and the dry, almost HR-approved praise of this one. It’s like saying, “He turned up on time and won’t swear on air”—which is faint praise on a show known for its mischief.

**How it Lands**  
Among general viewers, this joke is subtle and may not get a big laugh. It's more of a knowing chuckle, particularly for those familiar with the show's usual tone. It’s funny in its restraint and feels like a setup for further ribbing or contrast with the other flamboyantly described guests.

Comedy writers are likely to appreciate the tonal shift here. The conscious use of understatement makes it a “reverse” joke—funny because it sounds like the opposite of what you’d expect in this context.

**Funniness Ratings**  
Audience: 2 – A minor exhale out the nose  
Comedy writer: 4 – LOL  

# Jenni Falconer’s intro  
"Bill's second guest is GMTV's celebrity honey-trap and Scotland's answer to Lorraine Kelly, it's Jenni Falconer!"  

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction includes two distinct comedic elements: “celebrity honey-trap” and “Scotland’s answer to Lorraine Kelly.”

1. “Celebrity honey-trap” – This is the strongest joke. A “honey-trap” typically refers to someone used to seduce or lure someone into a compromising situation, often for surveillance or scandal. Referring to Jenni Falconer as GMTV’s “celebrity honey-trap” plays with this idea comedically, suggesting that her role on a light-hearted morning show was somehow seductive or manipulative, which is absurd and incongruous. It’s poking fun at the sometimes shallow or performative nature of morning television while exaggerating her role in an obviously ridiculous way.

2. “Scotland’s answer to Lorraine Kelly” – This joke works on a cultural reference. Lorraine Kelly is a beloved Scottish TV presenter known for her chirpy, warm persona. Saying Falconer is “Scotland’s answer” to her is humorous because Lorraine already is Scottish. The redundancy is the joke—it’s a logical paradox. Scotland doesn’t need an answer to Lorraine Kelly—she already is the answer. This redundancy highlights the absurdity of showbiz comparisons.

**How it Lands**  
The “celebrity honey-trap” line is likely to get a solid laugh from the audience, especially those familiar with the tone of GMTV and Falconer’s more glam appearance compared to the show’s content. The Lorraine Kelly reference is more of a slow-burn for those who catch the self-defeating logic.

Comedy writers will appreciate the layered wordplay and the knowingly ridiculous metaphors. The Lorraine Kelly bit has a classic “overexplanation” or tautology joke structure, which writers often enjoy crafting.

**Funniness Ratings**  
Audience: 4 – LOL  
Comedy writer: 5 – ROFL  

# David Gest’s intro  
"Phill's second guest is saucy funny-haired jungle stunner, it's David-reassuringly-expensive-Gest!"  

**Intended Humour**  
This is a rich, multi-layered joke packed with absurd descriptions and a name-based punchline.

1. “Saucy funny-haired jungle stunner” – This is a deliberately ridiculous string of adjectives:
   - “Saucy” is often used in tabloids to describe racy or flirtatious people—an absurd label for David Gest, a former music producer known more for his eccentricity than his sex appeal.
   - “Funny-haired” mocks Gest’s famously odd hairstyle, a detail audiences would recognize.
   - “Jungle stunner” refers to his appearance on I’m a Celebrity… Get Me Out of Here!, and “stunner” is again a tabloid term for an attractive person. Combined, they create a hilariously inappropriate glamorization of a man not typically described in such terms.

2. “David-reassuringly-expensive-Gest” – This is the punchline. It parodies the practice of inserting a humorous middle name into a celebrity’s name to satirize their persona. “Reassuringly expensive” is a phrase associated with luxury branding (once used in Stella Artois ads). Applying it to Gest suggests he’s both extravagant and somehow comforting in his poshness—a ridiculous and contradictory image. It’s the perfect climax to the build-up of absurd descriptors.

**How it Lands**  
This joke is a crowd-pleaser. The audience would find the absurd string of adjectives disarmingly funny, especially if they remember Gest’s eccentric public image. The “reassuringly expensive” bit is a smart cultural reference that adds depth for those who catch it.

Comedy writers would love this. It's a masterclass in comedic escalation, with increasingly ludicrous modifiers leading into a clever wordplay payoff. It’s a classic example of the “funny middle name” trope taken to a higher level with a layered cultural nod.

**Funniness Ratings**  
Audience: 5 – ROFL  
Comedy writer: 5 – ROFL

# Matt Priest's Intro

> "Phil's first guest, it's Matt Priest of Dodgy. Matt's such a legendary ligger that he was once unable to appear on this show, because he'd already accepted an invitation to the launch of the PG Tips triangular bag."

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction hinges on two layers of humor:  
1. The term "ligger" is British slang for someone who habitually attends parties and events solely for the free food, booze, or celebrity proximity—essentially a social parasite of the music/media circuit. Calling someone a “legendary ligger” is simultaneously an affectionate jab and a dig, suggesting Matt Priest is notorious for this behavior.
2. The punchline is that Matt couldn’t appear on a major music TV panel show (Never Mind the Buzzcocks) because he had already committed to a ridiculously minor event: the launch of the PG Tips triangular tea bag. The absurdity here lies in elevating a mundane, commercial product launch to something that would trump a TV appearance. 

The humor emerges from the juxtaposition of the platform (this show, associated with pop music prestige) and the petty event (a tea bag launch), implying that Matt either has terrible priorities or is such a committed freeloader that he can’t pass up even the most minor freebie opportunity.

**How it Lands**  
- For the typical British audience of Buzzcocks (who are usually music-savvy, pop-culture-aware, and enjoy sarcastic banter), this joke lands well. The reference to "liggers" is niche but well-known among those who follow the music/media scenes.
- The PG Tips triangular bag is a real product and had a bizarrely high-profile rollout in the UK, which adds a layer of absurd realism to the punchline.
- The joke pokes fun without being mean-spirited, and the image of prioritizing a tea bag launch over national television is comically disproportionate.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Audience at home: 4 – LOL. It’s a well-constructed joke with a satisfying punchline and a culturally specific reference that hits its target.  
- Comedy writer: 4 – LOL. The layered construction, cultural specificity, and absurdity of the payoff make it a strong example of a character-based intro gag.

# Tom Robinson's Intro

> "Phil's other guest is Tom Robinson, one of the finest pop writers to come out of punk. One of Tom's early hits was 'Glad to Be Gay.'"

**Intended Humour**  
This introduction appears largely factual. Tom Robinson is indeed a respected pop/punk musician known for his politically charged work, and “Glad to Be Gay” is one of his most famous songs. The delivery does not include a joke per se—it’s a straightforward statement of his credentials.

However, a subtle comedic undertone might be inferred from the way the song title is delivered deadpan. “Glad to Be Gay” was a controversial and groundbreaking song at the time, and the host’s casual mention of it could be seen as a dry nod to its boldness, particularly in the context of a light entertainment show. Still, this is more tonal than a formal joke.

**How it Lands**  
- To the audience, this doesn’t land as a joke. It’s informative and respectful, possibly even reverent.
- The lack of a laugh cue or ironic twist suggests the producers intended this one to be a breather or to avoid mocking a politically significant cultural figure.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Audience at home: 1 – Crickets. This is not intended to be funny.  
- Comedy writer: 1 – Crickets. There’s no real joke here to evaluate.

# Lauren Laverne's Intro

> "Sean's first guest, Lauren Laverne, singer with Sunderland's indie All Saints, Kenickie."

**Intended Humour**  
This joke relies on a deliberate misidentification:
1. Lauren Laverne was in Kenickie, a Britpop/indie band from Sunderland.
2. “All Saints” was a separate, highly successful mid-to-late '90s girl group with a polished R&B/pop sound, very much not “indie” and not from Sunderland.

The joke is in the absurd conflation of the two bands. It’s a mock error, delivered as if the host is clueless or deliberately misinformed. The humor comes from the incongruity between Kenickie’s gritty indie scene and All Saints’ sleek, commercial pop image—juxtaposing two wildly different acts, genres, and vibes as if they were the same.

It also plays on the idea of the host (Sean) being musically illiterate or indifferent—another recurring Buzzcocks trope. This reinforces his comedic character as someone who's only half-engaged with the show’s central subject: music.

**How it Lands**  
- This joke relies on audience familiarity with both bands. The Buzzcocks audience, being pop-culture savvy, is likely to get the reference and appreciate the deliberate absurdity.
- It’s a subtle joke, not a belly-laugh setup, but it rewards those who are in the know.
- Lauren Laverne herself, known for her intelligence and sharpness, is also likely to take the joke in stride, which adds to its warmth.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Audience at home: 3 – An audible snort. It’s clever and dry, but not sidesplitting.  
- Comedy writer: 3 – An audible snort. A smart, understated joke rooted in musical knowledge and character-based delivery.

# Jeff Green's Intro

> "Sean's other guest is Jeff Green, of TV and stand-up comedy fame. Jeff's been described in the press as a cheeky cherub, at least that's what he told the police when he was arrested on Hampstead Heath stark naked and holding a bow and arrow."

**Intended Humour**  
This is a fully-formed, classic Buzzcocks joke with a strong setup-punchline structure. Here's how it works:

1. Setup: "Jeff's been described in the press as a cheeky cherub." This is a plausible, if slightly flamboyant, description of a comedian—suggesting he’s impish, lovable, and mischievous.
2. Punchline: "At least that's what he told the police when he was arrested on Hampstead Heath stark naked and holding a bow and arrow."

The twist is that “cheeky cherub” isn’t a metaphorical media nickname, but a literal excuse Jeff gave to law enforcement after being caught in a highly incriminating (and ludicrous) scenario. The image of someone naked in a park with a bow and arrow is already absurd, but recontextualizing “cheeky cherub” as a defense elevates the humor.

It’s also a nod to public sex culture stereotypes associated with Hampstead Heath, a London park known (especially in the late 1990s and early 2000s) for clandestine meetups. This adds a slightly risqué edge, which is typical of Buzzcocks humor.

The joke functions through:
- Misdirection: The audience expects a mild anecdote about Jeff’s media persona.
- Escalation: It shifts to an outrageous visual punchline.
- Wordplay: The double use of “cheeky cherub” (figurative vs. literal) anchors the joke.

**How it Lands**  
- The absurdity and the risqué edge make this joke a standout. It fits Buzzcocks’ irreverent tone and penchant for slightly lewd, surreal humor.
- The audience likely laughs at the vivid mental image, combined with the escalation from flattering description to public indecency.
- Jeff Green, being a comedian, is a safe target for this kind of joke, and it's likely he was in on it or at least expected such ribbing.

**Funniness Ratings**  
- Audience at home: 5 – ROFL. Strong visual, perfect escalation, and fits the show's vibe.  
- Comedy writer: 5 – ROFL. Structurally sound, great timing, and excellent use of layered misdirection.

# Final Thoughts

This batch of introductions exemplifies the Buzzcocks house style: a blend of surreal, dry, and referential humor with a touch of affectionate roguishness. Every joke is either grounded in music culture or uses it as a springboard for broader comedy. The standout is Jeff Green’s intro for its perfect structure and vivid imagery, while Matt Priest’s is a close second for its social satire. Lauren Laverne's gets points for subtlety, while Tom Robinson’s is clearly a respectful, joke-free mention.
